As they watched, a tiny, hair-fine rootlet broke through a seam in the casing with an audible *pop*.

The machine was not being buried. It was being digested.

“The land knows its own,” Chidinma whispered, her voice full of a grim reverence.

Dike took a step forward, his engineer’s soul warring with the primal scene. “The power cell… if it breaches, the contamination…”

“The land will take that too,” Nnenna said. “It will be a different kind of poison. A slower one. But it will be of the earth, then. Not of the sky.”

Nkem approached the edge of the hungry mud. The chemical smell was stronger here, a metallic sting at the back of the throat. She saw something then, half-submerged in the ooze near the craft’s nose. Not a root. Something smooth and manufactured. A piece of the hull had torn away, and from within, a tangle of fiber-optic cabling spilled out, like gleaming, multicolored intestines. One cable, sheathed in brilliant blue, pulsed with a faint, internal light.

It was still alive. Some part of it, some isolated subsystem, was still drawing power, still signaling into the void.

Before she could speak, Dike saw it too. He lunged forward, his machete forgotten. “The black box! Or a beacon! We have to kill it!”

“Dike, no!” Nkem’s shout was sharp, but he was already leaning out, his hand stretching toward the pulsing blue cable.

The mud around his sinking foot *boiled*.

It was not a thermal boil. It was a violent upheaval, as if something large had turned over just beneath the surface. A gout of thick, sulfurous gas erupted, hitting Dike in the face. He choked, staggering back, his eyes streaming. From the disturbed patch of mud, a shape emerged.

It was a root, but a root like none Nkem had ever seen. It was the deep, burnished black of old blood, thick as her thigh, and studded along its length with nodules of crystalline silica that caught the grey light and broke it into sickly rainbows. It uncoiled from the muck with a slow, muscular grace, and with a wet, tearing sound, it wrapped itself around the spilled nest of cabling.

The pulsing blue light in the cable flared once, brilliantly, as if in a final protest. Then the black root constricted. There was a crunch of composite materials, a shower of tiny glass shards from the fiber optics, and the light went out, not with a fade, but with a decisive snap.

The root held its crushing embrace for a long moment, then slid back beneath the mud, leaving only a smooth, settling depression. The silence returned, deeper than before.

Dike retched, wiping his eyes. The smell of sulfur and burnt plastic hung over them.

“The land has its own teeth,” Nnenna said, her gaze fixed on the spot where the root had vanished. “You do not need to put your hand in its mouth.”

They retreated, the image of the black, crystalline root seared into their minds. It was a defender. A part of the Landmark’s extended biology, a white blood cell for an ecosystem. The technological anxiety Nkem felt now had a mirror: a biological ferocity, ancient and utterly alien to the logic of Lagos Freeport.

The rest of the afternoon was spent in a state of heightened vigilance. They saw no more signs from the forest, but the normal sounds did not return. The quiet was a held breath.

As the bruised grey sky began to darken into twilight, the second sign came. Not from the forest, but from the sky itself.

It began as a vibration in Nkem’s teeth, a high-frequency hum just at the edge of hearing. It was not the deep, organic thrum of the Landmark. This was a sterile, metallic whine. She looked up, searching the clouds.

Chidinma heard it too, her hand going to her flute. Dike stepped out into the compound, craning his neck.

Then, a light. Not a searchlight, but a brief, stuttering flash of intense white, somewhere high above the cloud ceiling. A silent flicker, like a distant sheet of lightning, but perfectly regular. Once. Twice. Three times.

“A skymarker,” Dike said, his voice hollow. “Grid-pulse positioning. They’re painting the area. Mapping the drop in the Scout’s signal to the meter.”

The flashes ceased. The whine faded. The silence that followed was not the forest’s silence. It was the silence of a search grid being drawn on a digital map, of coordinates being entered, of resources being allocated.

Nnenna stood in the doorway, a silhouette against the warm firelight of the hut. “They will come with the sun,” she said. “Not to search. To retrieve. They will bring tools for the mud. Cutters for the roots. They will not send a Scout next time. They will send a surgeon.”

That night, they did not sleep in the huts. At Nnenna’s direction, they took mats and thin blankets and moved to the *Ụlọ Mmụọ*, the Spirit House, at the edge of the compound. It was a small, circular building of mud-brick and thatch, older than Nnenna’s grandmother. Its air was cool and still, smelling of dried herbs, ancient clay, and peace. Here, the artifacts of Nnenna’s practice were kept: bundles of feathers, pots of rare earths, the carefully arranged bones of wise animals, the smooth river stones that held the memory of water.

It was a place of last resort.

Nkem lay on her mat, watching the shadows from the single oil lamp dance on the domed ceiling. The cold knot in her stomach had not dissolved, but here, in this close, sacred dark, it was joined by something else—a low, resonant warmth that emanated from the very walls. It was the Landmark’s presence, muted but palpable, a blanket of quiet power.

“We cannot fight surgeons with machetes,” Dike’s voice came from the darkness, taut with frustration. “We cannot hide a sinking ship. They will scan. They will find the alloy, the composites. They will drain the bog if they have to.”

“Then we must not let them see the ship,” Chidinma replied. Her voice was calm, almost dreamy. “We must make them see something else.”

“An illusion?” Dike scoffed. “Their machines will see through any camouflage we can make. They measure density. Thermal signatures.”

“Not an illusion,” Nkem said, the idea forming as she spoke, cold and clear. “A reflection. We cannot hide the corpse. But we can hide the cause of death. We can make the land itself tell a different story.”

Nnenna was silent for a long time. The only sound was the soft hiss of the lamp wick. When she spoke, her voice seemed to come from the walls themselves. “The song that brought down the Scout was a single note. A precise strike. To hide a thing… that is a different melody. A slower song. A song of forgetting. It requires a different instrument.”

“What instrument?” Nkem asked, propping herself up on an elbow.

Nnenna’s eyes gleamed in the low light. “The first instrument. The one that was here before the flutes, before the drums. The human vessel.” She looked at Nkem, then at Chidinma. “The land’s song is in its bones. To make the land lie, you must sing from a place deeper than truth. You must sing from the wound. You must let the land sing through you.”

A chill that had nothing to do with the night air traced Nkem’s spine. She remembered the vibration in her hands, the way the counter-melody had felt less like something she played and more like something that passed through her, wearing her thin as a reed. To do that again, not for a moment, but for hours… to become a conduit for a song of erasure…

“It will leave a mark,” Chidinma said softly, echoing Nkem’s thought.

“All true songs do,” Nnenna said. “The question is not the cost. The question is the alternative. Do you let the surgeon cut open the earth and remove its heart? Or do you let the earth make its heart invisible, even if it means your own heartbeat changes its rhythm?”

The decision hung in the herbal-scented air. It was no decision at all.

In the deepest hour of the night, they left the Spirit House. The moon was a smudge of tarnished silver behind the clouds. They did not go to the clearing. Instead, Nnenna led them to the base of the great Iroko tree that was the Landmark’s anchor, its roots like petrified rivers flowing into the dark earth.

Here, Nnenna prepared them. She used not herbs, but earth. She took damp clay from a specific pocket at the tree’s base, clay that was streaked with veins of fool’s gold and fine, black magnetite sand. In silence, she painted symbols on their foreheads, their throats, the centers of their palms. The clay was cold, and it drew the warmth from their skin greedily.

“This is not a shield,” Nnenna murmured, her thumb pressing the final mark over Nkem’s heart. “It is a conduit. A grounding. The song you will pull up is heavy. It is the weight of ages. Without the anchor, it will drown you. You will be an open door with no house attached.”

Chidinma and Nkem sat facing each other, their knees touching. Dike was positioned behind them, his hands on their shoulders, his role not to sing, but to hold, to be a tether to the mundane world.

“Begin not with the note,” Nnenna instructed, standing before them like a grim conductor. “Begin with the silence between the heartbeats. Find the emptiness in the mud, the patience in the stone. Then, let the Landmark fill it.”

Nkem closed her eyes. She let the compound’s sounds fade: Dike’s nervous breath, the distant rustle of leaves. She sought the silence Nnenna described. It was not an absence of sound, but a presence of a different order—the slow, vast hum of geological time. She felt for it in the cold clay on her skin, in the solid earth beneath her.

And then, she felt the Landmark.

It rose not as a melody, but as a *pressure*, a deep, subsonic swell that started in the bones of her pelvis and vibrated upwards. It was not music. It was pure, undifferentiated potential. The weight of ages.

Chidinma, opposite her, began to hum. It was a single, unwavering note, so low it seemed to be the audible edge of the pressure they felt. It was the tuning fork struck against the bedrock of the world.

Nkem opened her mouth. She did not decide what sound emerged. It was pulled from her, a raw, breathy tone that tangled with Chidinma’s hum. They were not singing a song. They were becoming the resonator for a frequency.

The air around them grew dense. The oil lamp’s flame guttered, then burned straight and tall, its light turning a pale, watery green. The painted clay on their skin grew warm, then hot, the magnetite grains within it aligning to an invisible field.

Nkem’s consciousness did not leave her body, but it expanded, painfully. She was aware of the roots beneath her, a vast, tangled network drinking from dark aquifers. She felt the slow, granular slide of the hillside, the patient compaction of strata. And she felt the foreign object, the Scout, a blister of wrongness in the flesh of the forest.

The song—the pressure—shifted. Guided not by their minds, but by the intent they had offered, it flowed toward that blister. It was not an attack. It was an overwhelming, smothering insistence.

In her mind’s eye, Nkem saw not images, but sensations. She felt the roots around the Scout not as vines, but as capillaries, and the song was a command in the sap: *Grow. Encase. Transform.* She felt the iron-rich mud not as soil, but as a viscous, intelligent fluid: *Swallow. Digest. Make same.* She felt the very air around the clearing thicken, its refractive index subtly altering: *Bend light. Scatter signal. Show nothing here.*

It was a symphony of erasure, conducted on a scale of molecules and milliseconds. She felt the Scout’s remaining energy signatures being smothered under a cascade of bio-electric noise from frenzied fungi. She felt its distinctive thermal profile being diluted into the general, damp warmth of decomposition. She felt the land learning to wear the machine’s corpse like a camouflage, incorporating its materials into a false history of slow decay.

The cost was immediate. It was a draining, not of energy, but of *self*. The song used their voices, their breath, their heartbeats as its instruments. Nkem felt a terrifying thinness, as if the essence of her—her memories of her mother’s laugh, the feel of sun on her shoulders, the specific weight of her own name—was being stretched over a vast frame, becoming translucent. She was a skin holding a ocean.

Dike’s hands on her shoulders were the only anchors. His grip was fierce, a desperate clamp on a reality that was slipping away. She could feel his fear, a bright, sharp spike in the overwhelming, ancient drone. It was a lifeline.

She did not know how long it lasted. Time became the rhythm of the song, which was the rhythm of tectonic plates. It could have been minutes. It could have been hours.

The song did not end. It receded, like a tide pulling back from a shore, leaving behind a world washed clean and strangely alien. The pressure lifted from Nkem’s chest. The heat in the clay symbols faded to a cool crust.

She opened her eyes. The world swam back into focus, but it was changed. The colors were muted, as if seen through a thin layer of ash. Every sound—Chidinma’s ragged inhale, Dike’s shuddering breath, the rustle of Nnenna’s wrapper—was preternaturally clear, yet distant, as if heard from the bottom of a well.

Chidinma looked at her, and Nkem saw a stranger. Her eyes were too old, their depths holding the echo of the deep earth’s patience. Her own face, she knew, would mirror it.

Nnenna helped them to their feet. Their bodies were stiff, cold. They moved like things newly assembled.

“It is done,” Nnenna said, her own voice hoarse. “The land has sung its lie. The corpse is now a fossil. The surgery will find only a historical curiosity, a machine lost long ago and slowly reclaimed.”

Dike stared at them, his face a mask of awe and horror. “What did it do to you?”

Nkem tried to speak. Her voice was a dry whisper, scraped raw. “It remembered through us.”

The walk back to the compound was a journey through a ghost world. The familiar huts seemed like stage sets. The dawn that was now bleeding into the east held no warmth. The technological anxiety was gone, not because the threat had passed, but because it had been swallowed by something vaster and more indifferent. They had not outsmarted the Authority. They had asked the earth to perform a magic trick, and it had, using pieces of their own souls as the sleight of hand.

As they reached the veranda, the first true sound of the new day reached them. Not birdsong. Not the wind.

It was the low, rhythmic, vibrating thrum of heavy rotors, still distant, but growing steadily louder from the southeast. A deep, mechanical heartbeat, approaching with the sun.

Nkem looked at her hands. The clay had cracked, falling away in flakes. Beneath it, her skin was unmarked. But she felt branded. She felt hollowed out, a vessel that had been filled with an ocean and then emptied, left with only the salt stain of the depths.

They had hidden the heart of the world. They had woven a lie from the truth of stone and root.

And now, the surgeons were coming to operate on a patient that would show them only a scar, while the real wound pulsed invisibly beneath, tended by keepers who had paid for the illusion with the sound of their own voices, and the light behind their own eyes.

The giant’s fingers were coming, precise and clinical, to probe the empty socket. And Nkem, who had helped pluck the eye out, now had to stand before them and swear the socket had always been empty.



---

# Chapter 16

## **CHAPTER 16**

The thrum was not a sound that arrived. It was a pressure that displaced the air, a physical weight that settled first in the hollow of the chest, then in the back teeth. It turned the morning haze into a trembling lens. Nkem stood on the veranda, her hands gripping the sun-warmed wood, and watched the southeast sky bleach to a pale, metallic grey. The approaching machines were not yet visible. They were a promise written in vibration, a bass note that made the laterite dust on the ground shiver into tiny, frantic dances.

Nnenna remained in her chair, her own hands still. The calabash of egusi seeds was forgotten at her feet. She watched the horizon with the flat, assessing gaze of a farmer measuring a coming storm. The ritual in the grove had carved new lines into her face, lines that looked less like age and more like the cracks in dry riverbeds, the kind that spoke of a profound and permanent thirst.

“They will be here before the sun clears the iroko,” she said, her voice still the raw whisper the clay had left them with. It carried no fear. It was a statement of topography, of inevitable geometry.

Dike leaned against a veranda post, his body a long line of tension. He had washed the clay from his skin, but his eyes held the same stunned, hollowed look. “The surgery. They will scan. They will take samples. They will ask questions whose answers are traps.”

“They will find a fossil,” Nkem said, repeating her grandmother’s lie. The words felt like stones in her mouth. “A machine reclaimed by the forest. A historical curiosity.”

“And us?” Dike’s question was a blade, thin and sharp.

Nnenna turned her head slowly. “We are the curiosity’s discoverers. Simple. Frightened of Authority. Helpful.” She paused, her gaze settling on Nkem. “The land has sung. Our part now is silence. The kind that looks like chatter. You must be empty for them. A calabash they can look into and see only their own reflection.”

To be empty. Nkem clutched at the feeling inside her—the vast, salt-stained hollow where the drone’s memories had flowed and then receded. It was not emptiness. It was occupancy by an absence, a shape left behind. She was a room where a deafening chorus had just finished singing; the silence it left was a specific, ringing thing. To make these surgeons see nothing in her… it would be like trying to hide a canyon by calling it a scratch.

The first shadow breached the horizon. It was a blunt, angular shape, trailing a long, insectile tail—a heavy lift transport, its six rotors churning the sky into a boiling soup of noise and downdraft. Two smaller, sleeker shapes flanked it, darting ahead like pilot fish. *Ụgbà elu*—Sky-Canoes. Attack drones. Their photoreceptors, even at this distance, glinted with a cold, blue intelligence.

The world shrank to the compound. The familiar sounds—the scrabble of chickens, the distant call of a hawker—were swallowed whole. The giant’s fingers were not subtle. They were an announcement. They were a declaration that this patch of earth, this cluster of huts and rusting metal roofs, was now a subject of intense, clinical interest.

By the time the transport settled in the fallow field beyond the last hut, its landing struts crushing cassava stems into green pulp, a crowd had gathered. A silent, watchful ring of neighbors, their faces carefully smoothed into masks of passive curiosity. No one approached. They stood at the perimeter where the rotor-wash became a mere stinging wind, their clothes snapping like flags.

A ramp descended from the transport’s belly with a hydraulic sigh. Figures emerged, silhouetted against the interior’s sterile light. They were not soldiers. They moved with a different precision. Three of them, clad in close-fitting grey coveralls devoid of insignia. Their boots were soft-soled, leaving precise, shallow prints in the mud. They carried no obvious weapons, only slender cases of brushed aluminum and sensor packs mounted on harnesses. The fourth figure was different—taller, wearing the crisp khaki and polished leather of a Provincial Authority Liaison Officer. His presence was the frame around the clinical picture.

The lead surgeon—Nkem knew instinctively that was their function—was a woman. Her hair was shorn to a shadow against her scalp. Her eyes, as she scanned the compound, were the colour of old ice. They passed over the crowd, over the huts, over Nkem on the veranda, with no more recognition than a scanner giving a first pass. She spoke to the Liaison Officer, her voice clipped, swallowed by the dying rotor noise.

The Officer nodded, then stepped forward, his boots making a show of crunching on the laterite. He produced a thin slate, its screen glowing.

“By order of the Provincial Authority, Sector Delta-Seven,” he announced, his voice amplified just enough to carry over the whispering crowd. “A signal anomaly has been logged and quarantined to this geographic locus. A Class-Two Archaeological-Security Protocol is now in effect. You will cooperate with the surgical audit team. There is no cause for alarm. Your compliance ensures your safety.”

*Surgical audit*. The term was cold, clean. It turned their grove, their ritual, into a pathology. The Liaison Officer’s eyes found Nnenna on the veranda. He approached, the surgeons following in his wake like a trio of silent, grey ghosts.

“You are the elder of this compound?”

Nnenna inclined her head, a slow, deep nod that was neither submission nor welcome. “I am Nnenna.”

“We were led to understand a device was found. A drone. You reported it to the local constable.”

“A child found it,” Nnenna said, her ruined voice forcing the Officer to lean slightly forward. “Broken. In the gully. We brought it here. It is dead.”

The lead surgeon’s ice-chip eyes flickered to Nkem. “You are the child?”

Nkem’s throat was a desert. She tried to summon moisture, to produce a tone that was not a rasp. “Yes.”

“Show me.”

It was not a request. Nkem led them, the procession feeling like a funeral in reverse, to the back of the main hut where the shattered *Mkpu* drone lay on a piece of sackcloth. They had not touched it since retrieving it from the grove. The *akịrịngba* thorns had left their deep, corrosive scores. Mud from the gully still caked its joints. It looked exactly as it should: a piece of sky-trash, brutally reclaimed by the earth.

The surgeons descended upon it. Their movements were a silent ballet. One opened an aluminum case, extracting a wand connected by a fibre-optic thread to a pad on her forearm. She passed it over the drone in slow, overlapping passes. A second surgeon placed small, disc-shaped sensors at the cardinal points around the wreckage. The third simply watched, her eyes recording everything—the drone, the ground, the way the morning light fell through the mango tree above, the tense set of Dike’s shoulders where he stood a few paces back.

The lead surgeon studied the readout on her forearm pad. Her expression did not change. “Total power failure predates physical damage. Core memory substrate is degraded. Organic infiltration is advanced.” She looked at Nnenna. “You handled it.”

“To bring it here,” Nnenna said.

“You touched the core housing.” It was a statement, pinpoint in its accuracy.

“It was broken open. We saw the dead lights inside. Like a crushed beetle.”

The surgeon’s gaze was a needle. “Did you experience any… perceptual disturbances? Auditory phenomena? Tactile hallucinations?”

Nnenna met the needle with stone. “It was a dead thing. It made no sound. It felt like cold plastic and torn wire.”

The surgeon held her eyes for a three-count, then looked back at her pad. “Residual signal analysis is negative. The anomaly signature has dissipated. The locus is clean.” She spoke to her team, not to them. “The reported pulse was likely a final capacitor discharge, amplified by the local geology. A phantom.”

The Liaison Officer seemed to relax a fraction. A phantom was a paperwork problem, not a security one.

But the watching surgeon, the silent one, spoke for the first time. Her voice was softer, almost melodic. “The degradation pattern is inconsistent.”

All movement stopped. The lead surgeon turned her head slowly. “Explain.”

The silent surgeon knelt. She did not touch the drone. She pointed a gloved finger at the grooves left by the *akịrịngba* vine. “Thorn scoring here, here, and here. Penetration suggests the vine grew *through* the housing after impact. Yet the corrosion at the fracture points…” She gestured to where the rotors had snapped. “This patterning suggests a chemical interaction far more accelerated than standard laterite or vegetative decay. Almost enzymatic.”

She stood, brushing red dust from her knee. Her eyes, a warmer brown than her colleague’s, swept over Nkem, Nnenna, Dike. “It is as if the environment here… digested it. With purpose.”

A stillness fell, deeper than before. The lead surgeon’s face remained impassive, but a new alertness tightened the skin around her eyes. She looked from the drone to the earth, to the surrounding forest that pressed in on the compound. “Run a full mineral and microbial spectrum. Take samples from the immediate soil, the vine, and…” her eyes landed on Nkem, “…epithelial swabs from the primary handlers. Cross-reference for catalytic agents.”

The Liaison Officer cleared his throat. “Is that necessary? You said the locus was clean.”

“The *signal* is clean,” the lead surgeon corrected, her voice frosty. “The method of dissolution is a data point. A unique one. The Provincial Authority catalogs all threats. A biological agent capable of targeted tech-degradation qualifies.”

They were no longer auditors. They were collectors. The grey ghosts moved with renewed purpose. One scraped soil into vials. Another clipped thorns from the vengeful *akịrịngba* vine with sterile shears. The third approached Nkem with a small, humming device.

“Your hand, please.”

Nkem extended her arm. The device was cool against her skin. It emitted a soft blue light that scanned her palm, her fingers, the space beneath her nails. It gave a discrete chime. The surgeon studied the readout. Her brow furrowed, a minute crack in the professional facade. She ran the scan again. The chime repeated.

“No residue,” she reported, her tone edged with confusion. “No trace polymers, no metallic micro-abrasions. Nothing.”

It was impossible. She had cradled the drone, had dug her fingers into its shattered core to place the memory seed. Her skin should have been a logbook of the contact. It was as clean as if it had been washed in aether.

The lead surgeon received this information with a slow blink. Her gaze travelled from Nkem’s clean hands to Nnenna’s weathered face, to Dike’s hollow eyes, to the quiet, watchful crowd at the edge of the field. The clinical certainty in her posture wavered, for a single, fleeting second, into something else—the recognition of a pattern that refused to resolve. A patient with no symptoms, yet a clearly diagnosed disease.

“The samples are sufficient,” she announced, her voice regaining its steel. “We will conclude.”

As her team packed their cases with a swift, efficient clatter, the silent surgeon lingered for a moment by the dead drone. She did not look at it. She looked at the ground where it lay, at the specific configuration of roots from the nearby mango tree, at the way a particular fern curled. Her head tilted, as if listening to a frequency below the hearing of machines or men. Then she turned and followed the others up the ramp.

The transport’s engines cycled up, the thrum building back into a world-drowning roar. The rotors bit into the air, churning the compound into a storm of red dust and flying chaff. The crowd raised arms to shield their faces. Nkem stood, unblinking, letting the grit sting her skin, watching as the giant’s fingers retracted into the belly of the machine, their curiosity unsatisfied, their data incomplete, but their instinct triggered.

The ramp closed. The transport lifted, its downdraft flattening the grass in a widening circle. The Sky-Canoes fell into formation. The noise began to recede, pulling away towards the southeast, leaving behind a ringing, battered silence.

The Liaison Officer remained. He brushed dust from his immaculate khaki sleeves. “Your cooperation is noted,” he said, his voice no longer amplified, almost conversational. “The file will be marked ‘Resolved – Environmental Anomaly.’” He paused, his eyes on Nnenna. “The forest here is… vigorous. You should be careful what you plant, old mother. Some seeds, once sown, draw attention from places you cannot prune.”

He gave a curt, meaningless nod and walked towards a sleek, ground-effect bike that had detached from the transport, leaving them alone in the settling dust.

The crowd began to disperse, melting back into the lanes between the huts, their silence now holding a new, wary texture. They had seen the Authority come, probe, and leave confused. It was not a victory. It was a postponement. A beast had sniffed at their door, found the scent strange, and withdrawn to ponder.

Dike let out a long, shuddering breath. “They believed it. The fossil story.”

“No,” Nnenna said, her voice a dry leaf in the quiet. “They believed their machines. Their machines told them a story they could file away. But the quiet one… she did not believe. She *saw*.”

Nkem looked at her hands, the skin unmarked, innocent. The land had not just hidden the memory. It had scoured her of the evidence. It had performed a deeper magic than they had asked for. They had offered themselves as conduits, as living clay to hold a lie. The earth had accepted their offering and then erased the marks of the transaction, leaving them holding nothing but the ghost of the bargain.

“They will be back,” Nkem said, the certainty of it cold in her hollow core. “Not the surgeons. Others. The ones who send the surgeons. They tasted a mystery. They felt the edges of the shape we made. They will send different fingers next time. Fingers that feel for pulses, not for scars.”

Nnenna nodded, a slow, weary descent of her chin. “The surgery is over. The fever will begin. We have hidden the heart, but the body is now warm with a false illness. They will treat the warmth.”

The rest of the day passed in a numb parody of normalcy. Nkem swept the veranda, the red dust from the transport mixing with the ordinary dirt. Dike tried to mend a fishing net, his fingers clumsy. Nnenna sat, not shelling egusi, just staring at the spot in the field where the landing struts had left deep, angry wounds in the earth.

As dusk bled the colour from the world, a different visitor came. It was Ugo, the old man who kept the shrine of the *mmuo* at the crossroads. He moved without sound, a wraith in indigo cloth. He did not approach the veranda. He stood at the edge of the compound, where the shadows pooled thickest, and raised a hand, palm outwards.

Nnenna rose and went to him. They spoke in tones too low to carry. When Nnenna returned, her face was graven from obsidian.

“The land spoke today, but not only to us,” she said, settling back into her chair with a weight that seemed final. “The shrine-keepers felt the… incision. The masking of the drone’s heart. They felt the earth swallow a piece of manufactured time. They say it has caused a dislocation. A ripple in the old ways.”

“What kind of ripple?” Dike asked, fear sharpening his voice.

Nnenna’s eyes found Nkem’s in the gathering dark. “The *mmuo* are restless. The paths between worlds have grown thin where we performed the weaving. The dead are closer. And they are curious about the new, quiet thing sleeping in the grove. Ugo says… he says they may try to speak to its keepers. To those who hear the memory of stone.”

A new dread, ancient and formless, seeped into the compound, colder than the technological anxiety of the Authority. They had used the deepest magic they knew, a song of root and rock. But every song echoes in more than one world. They had asked the earth to lie, and it had. But the truth it had hidden was not inert. It was a seed of alien memory, now buried in sacred ground. And the original inhabitants of that ground, the *mmuo*, the spirits of the place, had felt the foreign object’s burial, the strange, silent weight of its preserved past.

The Authority’s machines had seen a phantom. The land’s own ghosts had felt a transplant.

That night, sleep did not come. Nkem lay on her mat, the hollow inside her echoing with every rustle of the thatch, every cry of a night bird. The world had become a layered thing: the visible compound, the retreating mechanical eye of the Authority, and now this new, invisible layer of watchful, ancestral curiosity.

Just before the false dawn, when the darkness was at its most profound, she heard it. Not a sound, but a *pull*. A gentle, insistent tugging behind her navel, a compass needle swinging towards the forbidden grove. It was the memory seed. Not speaking. Not showing. Simply… present. And resonant.

It was a beacon, silent to machines, but now, somehow, tuned to the frequency of her own hollowed-out soul. And if she could feel its faint, persistent hum across the distance, a quiet, sleeping signal in the dark…

What else might be beginning to listen for it?

The giant’s fingers had retreated, clinical and confused.

But the forest’s own ears had now pricked up. And the thing they had buried was no longer just a secret to keep from the sky.

It was a note, held deep in the earth, waiting for a choir only she could hear to begin a response she did not know the words to.



---

# Chapter 17

## **CHAPTER 17**

The pull was not a sound, but a silence that had grown a gravity. It lived behind Nkem’s navel, a cold, smooth stone of awareness where her fear had been. She lay rigid on her mat until the false dawn bled its gray light through the cracks in the mud wall, staining the darkness the color of wood ash. The compound slept the thin, twitching sleep of hunted things, but the new tension was different. It did not scan. It listened.

She rose, the packed earth floor cold against her soles. On the veranda, the air tasted of damp clay and the lingering ozone of distant rain. Nnenna was already there, a silhouette against the gloom, her hands still. She was not shelling. She was facing the grove.

“You feel it,” the old woman said. It was not a question.

“It is like a hole in the world,” Nkem whispered. “A hole that… sings without a voice.”

“A hole draws things in. To fill itself.” Nnenna’s voice was the dry rustle of cassava leaves. “The *mmuo* are not evil. They are particular. They are the taste of this specific soil, the patience of this specific stone. You have planted a tooth from a different mouth in their gums. They will investigate the flavor.”

“Ugo said they might speak to its keepers.”

“Ugo speaks of spirits as if they are neighbors over the fence. They are not. They are the fence. And the land within it. To be noticed by them is to become part of the landscape. A tree does not converse. It simply is.” Nnenna finally turned. Her eyes in the half-light were like the drone’s dead photoreceptor, absorbing all light and giving none back. “The thing you pulled from the sky, this ‘seed.’ What does it want?”

“It wants to be remembered.”

“And you are the vessel for its remembering. So you are the path the *mmuo* will walk. They will come to understand the seed by understanding the soil it has chosen. Your soil, child. Your hollow places.”

The pull tightened, a subtle cinching of an invisible cord. It was not painful. It was precise. A summons written in a language of absence.

“I have to go to the grove.”

“I know.” Nnenna lifted a small, fibrous pouch from her lap, tied with vine. “Take this. *Nzu*. Chalk. And *uhie*. Camwood. Mark the boundaries of your self before you step into theirs. Your body is the only map you have in that conversation.”

Nkem took the pouch. The chalk was cool, the camwood powder warm, as if holding a sliver of moon and an ember from the same fire.

The walk to the forbidden grove was no longer a trespass of her own making. It was a procession. The forest was watchful, not hostile, but intensely observant. The morning birdsong seemed deliberate, each chirp placed like a bead on a string, leading her forward. The light did not fall through the canopy; it was portioned out, in narrow columns where dust motes turned like slow, celestial gears. The air smelled of decomposition and profound, green patience.

The clearing around the *ukwu* tree was different. The earth over the buried seed was not disturbed, but it had *settled*. It looked more natural than the surrounding ground, as if the memory pod had been there for decades, not days. The great roots of the tree seemed to have subtly shifted, a fraction of an inch, in a gentle embrace around the spot. The land was accepting the foreign body, digesting its presence.

Nkem stood at the edge of the clearing, the pouch heavy in her hand. The pull in her core was a steady, resonant thrum, in time with her own heartbeat. She knelt. With the white chalk, she drew a thin, shaky circle around herself on the leaf litter. With the camwood, she made a dot on her forehead, another over her heart. The red powder smelled of iron and old, sacred blood.

“I am here,” she said, not to the grove, but to the pull within her. “Show me.”

Nothing happened with the violence of vision or dream. The world did not warp. Instead, it *clarified*.

The sound of the leaves above was not random. It resolved into patterns, slow and vast as continental drift. The murmur of water in the distant stream became a low, continuous vowel of the earth. And beneath it all, underneath the hearing, was a sense of attention. It was not focused on her chalk circle. It was focused on the point beneath the soil, and the line that ran from that point to the center of her own chest.

A presence gathered. Not a shape, but a *quality*. The feeling of deep shade on a hot day. The taste of water from a clay pot. The sound of a stone turning over in a riverbed, once a century. It was the essence of *here*, of this specific confluence of rock and root and time.

It pressed against the edges of her perception. It had no words. It had inquiries. They arrived as impressions, as comparisons.

*The-not-tree. The-silent-stone. The-dream-that-is-not-of-river-or-rock.*

It was assessing the memory seed. Nkem understood this not through thought, but through a direct knowing, as one knows hunger or thirst. The *mmuo* perceived the pod as an inert, non-living thing that nonetheless contained a torrent of non-organic memory. A stone that dreamed of electric skies and silent, thinking metal. A profoundly alien artifact.

Then, the attention shifted. From the seed, to the thread, to her.

It was a gentle, terrifying scrutiny. She felt it trace the hollow in her spirit, the place where her father’s absence and her mother’s silence had carved out a chamber. It did not judge the hollow. It measured its capacity. It noted the echoes within it—the whine of drones, the static of lost transmissions, the sound of her own breath in a too-quiet house.

*You-are-the-vessel. You-hold-the-silence-that-calls-to-this-not-dream.*

An understanding was formed, not offered, but simply established, as a fact of the landscape. She was a feature. The seed was a feature. Their connection was a path. The *mmuo* did not care about the Provincial Authority, about secrecy or survival. They cared about equilibrium. A new thing had been introduced. It must be integrated. The integration would occur through the available conduit.

Her.

The presence began to recede, not vanishing, but returning to its background state of slow, geological perception. But as it did, it left something behind. Not a message, but a permission. Or perhaps an adjustment.

The pull in her navel did not fade. It changed. It became bidirectional. She could still feel the seed’s silent, resonant hum. But now, she could also feel… the land’s reception of it. A faint, vibrational echo. The memory pod was broadcasting its captured past into the earth, and the earth, in its own immense, slow language, was humming back.

The grove was no longer just hiding a secret. It was *processing* one.

Nkem stood, her legs stiff. The chalk circle was smudged. The camwood on her skin felt like a brand. She walked back to the compound, the forest’s watchfulness now a settled fact against her skin, like a change in atmospheric pressure.

Ugo was waiting by the compound gate, his usual levity sanded away. He looked at her face and took a half-step back.

“You went. They spoke.”

“They didn’t speak,” Nkem said, her voice sounding foreign to her own ears, smoothed by the presence of that vast, quiet attention. “They… categorized.”

She told him. The hollow as a vessel. The seed as a not-dream. The integration.

Ugo’s face grew long with a dread that was older than the dread of soldiers or scanners. “This is deep juju, Nkem. Deeper than my tricks. They are making you a part of the story of that land. If the Authority comes back with their machines…”

“The machines will see a phantom. The land will see an imbalance. And it will act to correct it.” She looked down at her hands. “Through me.”

“Then we must understand the seed’s story *before* the land decides how to tell it,” Ugo said, a desperate logic seizing him. “You are the reader. You must read faster than the earth can interpret.”

That afternoon, they went to the shed. The dead Mkpu drone lay on the bench where Nkem had left it, a skeleton of a different world. Its quiet was merely technical, a shallow silence. It held no gravity.

Nkem connected the salvaged reader to her battered tablet. The screen flickered, the corrupted data from the memory seed a storm of static and fragmented glyphs. Before, it had been noise. Now, feeling the seed’s resonant hum in her core, the noise had a texture. It had a rhythm that matched the new, bidirectional pull.

“It’s not just broken,” she murmured, her fingers hovering over the screen. “It’s… encrypted. But not with a code. With a context.”

“A context?”

“It needs to be heard in the right… place. In the right state.” She thought of the *mmuo*’s assessment. *The-dream-that-is-not-of-river-or-rock.* This was a memory of metal and vacuum. To understand it, perhaps she had to meet it halfway. Not on the land’s terms, nor on its own, but in the hollow space between.

An idea, reckless and crystalline, formed. “The grove is listening to the seed. The seed is broadcasting. What if… I listen in the grove? With the land’s attention as a… a filter? A translator?”

Ugo stared at her. “You want to use the spirit of the place as a data-decryption tool?”

“You said it yourself. All juju is technology. Maybe all technology is just juju that has forgotten the land it’s built on.”

He had no argument. The old categories were crumbling.

As dusk bled across the sky, Nkem returned to the grove. She did not draw a circle this time. She sat with her back against the *ukwu* tree, its bark solid and real against her spine. She placed the tablet in her lap, the reader connected to it by a thin, black cable that looked absurd against the leaf litter. She closed her eyes.

She focused on the pull. Not just on the seed’s signal, but on the land’s echo. She let the vibrational hum fill the hollow inside her. She became the conduit, not trying to grasp either side, simply allowing the channel to exist.

Then, she opened her eyes and activated the reader.

The screen did not resolve into clean images. It did not play a recording.

It *pulsed*.

Waves of light and dark, synchronized with the thrum in her navel. Between the pulses, in the fleeting intervals of not-light, shapes coagulated. Not full pictures, but impressions, bleeding through the static like blood through bandages.

A curved wall, impossibly smooth, reflecting a light that was neither sun nor bulb. A glimpse of a figure in a suit that was not fabric, its surface a matte, non-reflective gray, moving with a fluid, weightless grace. A console alive with symbols that danced like fireflies, arranging and rearranging themselves into geometries that hurt her mind to follow. A viewport. And beyond it, not the blackness of space with stars, but a swirling, luminous nebula of colors that had no name—a sight that was less a visual experience and more a direct injection of awe and terror into the soul.

This was not a crash. This was a… departure. A controlled, deliberate shedding. The memory was not of destruction, but of a ritual unloading. The pod had been *ejected*. Purged. It contained not a record of a disaster, but a cargo. A selected archive. A dying god’s last breath, captured in crystal and silicon.

The emotion embedded in the fragments was not panic. It was a profound, sorrowful resolve. A farewell.

The image that lasted longest, burning itself into the screen and her mind’s eye, was a hand—long-fingered, with too many joints—pressing against the inside of the viewport. Not in distress. In a gesture of… transfer. Of bestowal. The pod was not a black box. It was a reliquary.

The pulse intensified. The hum in her bones became a vibration that threatened to shake her teeth loose. The land’s echo was no longer just receiving; it was *responding*. The *mmuo*, perceiving this torrent of alien memory, this not-dream of metal and farewell, began to react. The air in the grove grew thick, charged. The leaves of the *ukwu* tree above her began to tremble, though there was no wind.

The clearing was no longer processing quietly. It was engaging.

A root, thick as Nkem’s thigh, shifted in the earth near the buried pod with a sound like grinding teeth. The soil above the memory seed cracked open, not violently, but with the inevitable, slow motion of a flower turning towards a sun. The pod was not pushed out. It was *presented*.

It lay in its shallow cradle of earth and root, its obsidian surface now filmed with a fine network of glowing, bioluminescent fungus—a mycelial net the forest had grown in days, a living interface attempting to commune with the alien artifact.

The tablet screen went black. The connection severed, the reader overheating in her hand.

Nkem sat, gasping, the visions seared into her. The grove was alive with a soft, fungal light that pulsed in the same rhythm she had felt. The memory pod was no longer hidden. It was on display, halfway between the earth that held it and the air that sought it, being studied by a intelligence of root and rot.

She had wanted to read the story. The land, now a co-reader, had turned the page. And in doing so, it had announced their discovery not to the sky, but to the deeper fabric of the world itself.

The integration had begun. And she, the vessel, was now full to the brim with a silent, screaming history that was not hers, witnessed by a consciousness that predated bones. The secret was no longer buried.

It was in conversation.

And she was the language in which it spoke.



---

# Chapter 18

## **CHAPTER 18**

The fungal light died first. It did not fade. It was there, a soft, pulsing cerulean net across the obsidian pod and the roots that cradled it, and then it was not. The severance was absolute. One moment, the grove was a living circuit, a node of deep, bioluminescent communion. The next, it was just a clearing in the false dawn, dark and ordinary and cold.

The silence that followed was not an absence of sound. It was a presence. It was the sound of a held breath, of a world listening back to a signal that had just gone dead. The hum in Nkem’s bones did not dissipate; it inverted. It became a hollow resonance, a cavity where the alien memory had been. She was not empty. She was a shaped void, a vessel scoured clean by a story too vast to hold. The after-images flickered behind her eyes—the silver city, the silent song of the last keeper, the falling away into the black. They were not memories. They were ghosts inhabiting the architecture of her mind, and they brought with them the chill of interstellar vacuum.

She sat, paralyzed, the dead tablet a cold slab on her lap. The *ukwu* tree above her was still. The root that had presented the pod now seemed just a root, a gnarled, mundane thing half-buried in dirt. The pod itself was merely an object again, a black egg smeared with common soil, the glowing mycelium vanished as if it had never been.

She had thought the land was reading with her. She had been wrong. The land had been *interrogating*. And it had reached a conclusion.

A hand touched her shoulder. She did not startle. The touch was of this world, familiar and solid. It was Udo. His face, seen in the bleary grey light, was etched with a fatigue deeper than sleeplessness. He had been watching from the edge of the grove.

“It stopped,” he said. His voice was hoarse. “The… feeling. It just stopped.”

Nkem tried to speak. Her tongue was a dry leaf. She managed a nod.

“What did you see?” he asked, not demanding, but dreading.

“The end of them,” she whispered. The words were ashes. “Not a war. A choice. A… surrender. To silence.” She looked at the pod. “It’s not a record. It’s a tombstone. And we just invited the forest to read the epitaph.”

Udo’s eyes widened slightly. He understood implications faster than most. “And the forest didn’t like the story.”

Before she could answer, a new sound threaded through the morning. Not the digital chirp of a drone, not the organic rustle of the *mmuo*. This was mechanical, terrestrial, and profoundly out of place. The distant, grinding growl of heavy-duty diesel engines, punctuated by the sharp *snap* of branches being forced aside.

Nkem was on her feet, the cold numbness burning away in a flash of pure, animal alarm. Udo was already moving, pulling her back into the deeper shadow of the *ukwu*. They knew that sound. It was the sound of the Provincial Authority’s terrain vehicles, the ones they used for deep-sector resource surveys and, occasionally, for punitive clearances. They were not meant for the delicate, vine-strangled paths of the forest. Their presence here was a violation, a declaration.

The vehicles were not approaching the grove. They were coming from the direction of the town.

They moved through the forest like hunted things, Nkem’s senses stretched thin. The world had turned brittle. The usual sounds—the chatter of monkeys, the sigh of the canopy—were absent, swallowed by the aggressive mechanical din from the east. The *mmuo* was not silent; it was coiled, watchful. The air tasted of turned soil and diesel exhaust.

They reached the tree line overlooking Nkem’s compound from the west. The sight there was not of violation, but of eerie, formalized intrusion.

Three Authority vehicles, boxy and mud-spattered, were parked in the clearing where the old cocoa sheds had stood. Their engines were idle, but their presence was a shout. A dozen figures in the khaki and grey of Provincial Security moved with a methodical, insectile purpose. They did not rush. They did not shout. They were not searching.

They were cataloging.

One team was at the veranda, where Nnenna sat perfectly still in her chair. A security officer, a woman with a scanner in hand, was politely, coldly, inventorying the contents of the shelves: the jars of herbs, the bundles of roots, the old tools. Another officer was taking high-resolution photographs of the house’s corrugated metal walls, the patterns of rust, the placement of windows.

A second team was by the *ogbu* tree. A man in the crisp uniform of a Logistics Officer was directing two others as they carefully, almost reverently, placed Nkem’s salvaged drones—the Mkpu, the older Falcon, the others—into padded crates. Not seizing. Collecting.

At the center of it all, standing with his hands clasped behind his back, was a man Nkem had only ever seen on low-resolution civic announcement streams: Administrator Folami. He was shorter in life, his face a careful mask of benign concern. He was watching the operations, occasionally nodding. Beside him, holding a slate and speaking in a low, continuous murmur, was Dr. Eze. The academic’s earlier agitation was gone, replaced by a focused, scholarly intensity. He gestured towards the forest, towards the grove, his words clearly explaining, contextualizing, guiding.

Nkem’s blood turned to ice. It was not a raid. It was an audit. And Eze was providing the footnotes.

Udo’s hand tightened on her arm. “The signal,” he breathed. “The pod’s pulse, the land’s echo… it wasn’t hidden. Eze’s equipment at the station. He must have detected the surge. Refined the location.”

Nkem understood. Their victory—the integration, the communion—had been a beacon. They had not hidden the secret. They had amplified it, tuned it to a frequency the Authority’s listening posts could finally lock onto. The land’s conversation had been overheard.

She watched as Administrator Folami turned and spoke to Nnenna. He bowed slightly, a gesture of respect that was utterly obscene in this context. Nnenna did not look at him. She looked past him, her eyes finding the tree line where Nkem hid with an uncanny precision. Her face showed no fear, no anger. It showed a profound, weary recognition. She gave the faintest shake of her head. *Do not come.*

Then, Folami’s gaze followed Nnenna’s. He did not see Nkem, but he looked directly at the space she occupied. He smiled, a small, official thing. He knew she was there. He was waiting.

“They want you to walk in,” Udo whispered, the horror dawning. “This is all for you. A presentation.”

The choice was a knife in her gut. To go out was to step into their narrative, to become a component in their investigation. To stay hidden was to let them continue their polite dissection of her life, her grandmother’s life, under the sun.

Nkem stepped out of the trees.

The activity did not cease, but it quieted. Eyes tracked her. Dr. Eze’s monologue faltered. Administrator Folami’s smile widened, becoming almost paternal.

“Miss Nkem,” he called, his voice carrying across the compound with practiced ease. “We were beginning to worry. Please, join us. No need for alarm. This is a protective operation.”

She walked towards him, the red earth familiar under her feet, now feeling like a stage. She stopped a few paces away, ignoring Folami, her eyes on Dr. Eze. “You traced my resonance.”

Eze had the decency to flinch. “It was… a seismic anomaly. Unprecedented. Localized to this coordinates. Coupled with your earlier physiological data, the correlation was… protective custody was deemed necessary.” The words were bureaucratic, but his eyes were alive with a hungry, terrified fascination. “What happened in the grove, Nkem? The readings… they spiked and then nullified completely. What did you *do*?”

Before she could answer, Folami raised a placating hand. “All in good time, Doctor. Miss Nkem, you must understand. What you have been dabbling in here… it is no longer a matter of local folklore or salvage rights.” He gestured to the crated drones. “Your unique talents have, it seems, attracted something from beyond our regulatory frameworks. A foreign object of unknown origin and capability. The Provincial Authority has a duty to secure it, and to ensure the safety of the citizen who discovered it.” He took a step closer, his voice dropping to a confidential tone. “You are not in trouble. You are a resource. A vital one.”

The word hung in the air. *Resource*. Like the lithium sands to the north. Like the old-growth timber. Something to be measured, extracted, and utilized.

Nnenna spoke from the veranda, her voice dry and clear as a stone dropping in a well. “You wrap theft in a blanket of safety, Administrator. You always do.”

Folami’s smile didn’t slip. “Madam Nnenna, I assure you, this is for the greater stability. An unregistered, active extraterrestrial artifact represents a clear and present danger. Its energy signatures are interfering with local… well, with local ecological balances.” He chose the words carefully, but his glance at the forest betrayed him. He knew. Perhaps not the depth of the *mmuo*, but he knew the artifact was talking to *something*, and that something made his regulatory soul deeply uneasy.

“Where is the artifact now?” Dr. Eze could not contain himself.

Nkem looked at him. “The forest has it.”

A flicker of confusion crossed Folami’s face. Eze’s, however, lit with understanding and dread. “Integration. You facilitated a bio-interface.” It was not a question.

“It was reading the memory,” Nkem said. “It finished. The light went out.”

“Show us,” Folami commanded, the paternal tone gone.

The procession to the grove was a funeral march. Authority personnel flanked Nkem, their boots crushing delicate undergrowth. Eze and Folami walked behind her, followed by two security officers carrying a heavy, shielded container. Nkem felt the land’s disapproval like a pressure change. The *mmuo* was present, but distant, retreating into deeper layers of reality, leaving behind only a residue of watchful hostility.

When they entered the clearing, the ordinariness of the scene was its own kind of shock. There was the pod, resting in its cradle of roots. There was no light, no vibration, no sign of the transcendent communion of hours before. It was inert. A stone.

Folami looked unimpressed. “This is it?”

Eze was already on his knees, scanner whirring. “No residual energy. No emissions. It’s… dormant. Or depleted.” He looked up at Nkem, desperate. “What did it show you?”

“A farewell,” she said simply.

Folami nodded to the officers. “Secure it. Extreme caution.”

The two men approached the pod with the stiff care of bomb technicians. They slid composite straps under the root-cradle. As they began to lift, the root itself did not resist. But the earth did.

It was not an attack. It was a release.

The soil around the pod, for a radius of three meters, simply… liquefied. It did not collapse. It became fluid, black water, swallowing the officers’ boots to their shins. They cried out, stumbling, the pod lurching in its straps. The *ukwu* tree gave a single, deep groan, like a great ship settling. From the liquified earth, a smell erupted—not of decay, but of profound fermentation, of knowledge turned to vinegar, of memory becoming rot.

The pod, as if weighed down by this sudden mire, settled deeper, tilting. One of the officers lost his grip. The pod slid, not with a crash, but with a thick, sucking sound, and came to rest half-submerged in the black slurry.

The grove had not attacked. It had withdrawn its support. It had made the artifact untakeable.

Folami’s face was pale with fury. “Get it out! Use the vehicle winch!”

“Administrator,” Eze said, his voice trembling with a different understanding. He was pointing his scanner not at the pod, but at the trees, at the air. “The nullification is spreading. It’s not just the artifact. The entire area’s… background resonance. The low-level electromagnetic field that’s always here… it’s gone. It’s a dead zone.”

Nkem felt it. The hollow in her bones was now outside her. The grove was not just silent. It was *empty*. The *mmuo* had not just retreated; it had severed the connection. It had cut this place out of its body, like a surgeon excising a contaminated cell.

The land, having listened to the alien silence, had learned it. And now it was reproducing it.

While Folami barked orders, trying to get a winch cable hooked to the mired pod, Nkem backed away. No one stopped her. Their focus was on the impossible mud, the inert prize. She melted back into the trees, not towards the compound, but deeper into the forest, towards the one place she knew the Authority would not yet have touched: the relay station.

Udo was already there, his face ashen. He had followed at a distance. “They can’t move it,” he said.

“The land won’t let them,” Nkem replied, her mind racing. “It’s made the pod a part of its own dead zone. But that’s not the problem.” She pushed past him into the dim hut. The reader tablet was on the workbench, still dead. She connected it to a portable power cell. The screen flickered, then showed a system error. The memory file was corrupted. Not deleted. Corrupted. As if the act of communal reading had rewritten the data into a form only a forest could understand.

“What is the problem?” Udo asked.

“Eze. He doesn’t have the pod, but he has all the preliminary data. The resonance signatures, my biometrics, the location. He has the *theory* of it. And Folami has the authority.” She turned to face him, the full, crushing weight of the false victory settling upon her. “We didn’t keep the secret safe, Udo. We gave them the key to understanding what the secret *is*. They don’t need the pod anymore. They know the artifact talks to the land. And now they know the land can be… persuaded… to reject it. To create dead zones.”

She saw the understanding dawn in his eyes, followed by horror. The Provincial Authority didn’t just want to study an alien artifact. They wanted to understand the mechanism of its interaction with the *mmuo*. To quantify it. To control it. The ultimate technological anxiety: not fear of the alien, but the desire to weaponize the interface between the alien and the ancient.

“They’ll try to replicate it,” Udo said, his voice hollow. “To induce the silence.”

Outside, the diesel roar of the winch started, a mechanical scream against the new, profound quiet of the grove. It was the sound of one world trying to extract a tooth from another. It would not succeed today. But it was just the beginning.

Nkem looked at her hands, the hands that had carried the dead drone, that had touched the pod, that had become a conduit. She had been the language. Now, the conversation was over, and the words she had spoken had been recorded, transcribed, and filed by men in khaki uniforms. The secret was not just in conversation.

It was in a report. And a report was a seed for a different kind of integration—one of steel cables, seismic charges, and a silence imposed not by choice, but by decree.

The winch engine strained. A root snapped with a sound like a breaking bone.

The deeper truth, the one the lens of technological anxiety revealed, was this: the greatest danger was never the alien memory. It was the human understanding of it. They had not uncovered a mystery. They had blueprinted a weapon. And the forest, in its wisdom, had already begun its own, terrible quarantine.

The hook was set. The extraction had begun. Not of the pod, but of the very possibility of communion.



---

# Chapter 19

## **CHAPTER 19**

The silence was not an absence. It was a presence. It had texture, weight, a low-frequency hum that vibrated in the marrow of Nkem’s teeth and made the fillings ache. It was the sound of the *mmuo* holding its breath, a vast, living entity recoiling from a burn. The grove around the extraction site was not dead. It was listening. And what it heard was the diesel-throated winch, the shriek of stressed polymer cable, the shouted, technical language of men who spoke of tensile strength and substrate integrity.

They stood at the edge of the new clearing, Nkem and Udo, behind a cordon of faded yellow tape that fluttered in a breeze that did not touch the trees. The Provincial Authority had brought in a mobile lab, a repurposed shipping container on treads, its sides studded with antennae and environmental sensors. A cable, thick as a man’s wrist, snaked from a powered winch on a tracked vehicle, down into the raw wound in the earth where the pod lay. The laterite, exposed, looked like a blood clot.

“They are not pulling it out,” Udo murmured, his eyes fixed on the straining cable. “They are asking the earth to vomit it.”

His words were precise. The winch engine would roar, the cable would go taut, singing a single, high note of protest. The ground would shudder. Then, a deep, subsonic groan would emanate from the soil itself, a sound felt more than heard. The cable would slacken. The winch would roar again. It was a conversation of brute force and deep, geological refusal.

Nnenna had not come. She was in her hut, with the calabash of egusi seeds, her hands moving in that ancient, click-clack rhythm. A different kind of pulling. A different kind of waiting.

A man in a crisp, short-sleeved khaki shirt and data-glasses stood by the winch controls, a tablet in his hands. Dr. Alabi. He was from the Lagos Freeport’s Xenostudies Directorate, a man whose anxiety was not spiritual but bureaucratic. The failure to extract was a mark on a report, a delay in a schedule. He glanced at the tablet, then at the hole, his mouth a thin line of impatience.

Nkem watched him. This was the lens made flesh. Technological anxiety, incarnate as a man with a budget and a performance metric. His fear was not of the unknown, but of the unknown’s resistance to quantification. Of its refusal to be a data point.

“The damping field is still active,” a technician called from the open door of the mobile lab, her voice tinny through a headset. “Readings are consistent with the earlier event profile, but localized. It’s clinging to it. Like a… a magnetic biofilm.”

Dr. Alabi made a note. “Increase power to the winch by ten percent. And prepare the resonant dislocators.”

Udo stiffened beside her. “Resonant dislocators. They are going to try to shatter the bond. To give the land a sonic headache until it lets go.”

The winch’s roar deepened, becoming a predatory snarl. The cable trembled, shedding red dust. This time, the groan from the earth was sharper, pained. A crack appeared in the laterite, zigzagging away from the pit like a forked tongue. From the fissure, a smell emerged—not of damp soil, but of ozone and crushed green stems, the scent of a living system rupturing.

Then, the forest answered.

It began with the *akịrịngba* vines. They descended from the canopy at the clearing’s edge not with a rustle, but with a coordinated, silent plunge. Thorned tendrils, each as thick as a finger, lashed around the treads of the winch vehicle, around the legs of the mobile lab. They did not creep. They struck.

Technicians shouted, leaping back, hacking at the vines with machetes drawn from belts. The thorns scored deep grooves in the synthetic rubber of the treads, in the painted metal of the lab. It was not an attack aimed at flesh, but at machine. The vines sought purchase, tightening with a slow, relentless strength that made the winch vehicle list to one side.

Dr. Alabi stumbled back, his tablet falling to the mud. “Clear them! Use the herbicide spray!”

A man in environmental gear wrestled with a tank and a nozzle. A jet of milky chemical mist hissed out, coating the vines. They writhed, a slow, agonized motion, and their thorns wept a clear, viscous sap that smoked where it met the herbicide. They did not retreat. They tightened further, and the winch vehicle groaned on its suspension.

Nkem felt it then—a pulse through the soles of her feet. A directive. Not words, not images. A pure, vectored intention, flowing from the deep place where the pod dreamed, channeled through the mycelial networks, the root systems, the secret waterways. It was the land, coordinating a defense. Not with thought, but with instinct. A body rejecting a splinter.

The *akịrịngba* were the neutrophils. The silence was the swelling.

“It’s targeting the technology,” Udo whispered, awe and horror twisting his features. “It’s reading the emissions, the vibrations, the electromagnetic spill. It knows what is foreign. What is *ask*.”

Dr. Alabi had retrieved his tablet, wiping mud on his khaki trousers. His face was alight not with fear, but with a fervent, clinical excitement. He was watching the forest’s response, his fingers flying over the screen, recording, analyzing. “Fascinating! Targeted phytological aggression. Non-random. It’s defending the artifact. Note the specificity—the winch, the lab. It’s ignoring the personnel unless directly threatened. This is a systemic immune response.”

He didn’t see a spirit fighting back. He saw a mechanism. A puzzle. And in that moment, Nkem understood the true depth of the hook that was set. They had not come for the pod. They had come for *this*. For the reaction. The forest was performing its own defense, and in doing so, it was revealing its tactics, its capabilities, its points of vulnerability. Every vine that struck, every pulse of silencing field, was another data point for Dr. Alabi’s report.

The winch engine coughed, sputtered, and died. The vines had found the exhaust port, clogging it with their thorny mass. In the sudden quiet, the hum of the mobile lab’s generators seemed obscenely loud.

“Switch to auxiliary power,” Dr. Alabi ordered, his voice crisp. “And deploy the drones. I want a full spectral map of this response. I want to see the command signal.”

From the roof of the lab, a hatch slid open. Four drones, sleek and black, ascended on near-silent ducted fans. They were not the cheap, skeletal *Mkpu* models. These were military-grade, shaped like manta rays, with clusters of sensor eyes that glinted in the filtered light. They fanned out, hovering at the tree line, their undersides glowing with soft, scanning luminescence.

The forest went still. The vines ceased their writhing. It was a watchful, calculating stillness.

The drones began to pulse, emitting a rapid, clicking chatter of lidar and directed sonar. Painting the grove in invisible light, building a real-time model of every branch, every root, every thermal signature.

The response was instantaneous, and of a different order.

The air around the drones *thickened*. It was a visible distortion, like heat haze off the laterite, but cold. The humming in Nkem’s teeth spiked into a needle of pain. The drones’ smooth hover became a jerk, a stutter. One listed sideways, its fan whining erratically. Another simply dropped two feet, as if the air beneath it had turned to water, before righting itself.

“They’re entering the damping field,” the technician called from the lab, her voice rising. “It’s not just around the pit anymore. It’s… projecting. It’s targeting the drones’ propulsion and sensor suites. Signal degradation is catastrophic.”

On Dr. Alabi’s tablet, the crisp, three-dimensional map of the grove dissolved into static, then reformed as a ghostly, fragmented outline. The drones were blind, their electronic minds scoured by the same silence that protected the pod.

“Recall them,” Dr. Alabi said, a flicker of his earlier anxiety returning. Not fear of the forest, but fear of losing expensive equipment.

The drones turned, struggling against the thickened air, flying drunkenly back toward the lab. As the first one crossed back over the tree line, the distortion faded. Its flight smoothed. The map on the tablet flickered back to partial clarity.

Dr. Alabi stared at the data, then at the grove, then at the pit. His expression was one of pure, avaricious understanding. “It’s not a field. It’s a *weapon*. A directed, adaptive, area-denial weapon. It can localize its effect. It can distinguish between targets.” He turned to a subordinate, his eyes bright. “Shut everything down. The winch, the lab, all non-essential emissions. I want to see the baseline. I want to see what it considers ‘non-threatening.’”

The order was given. The generators wound down with a dying whine. The winch was silent. The mobile lab went dark, its antennae retracting. The sudden cessation of mechanical noise was profound. Only the drip of water from leaves, the distant call of a hornbill, the rustle of the still-tense vines remained.

The silence of the *mmuo* softened, but did not dissipate. It became a watchful hum again. The vines slowly retracted, slithering back into the canopy, leaving deep, weeping scratches on the machines.

Dr. Alabi stood in the mud, his khaki shirt stained, and he smiled. It was a thin, triumphant smile. “There,” he said softly, to no one and everyone. “You see? It has rules. It has parameters. It can be mapped. It can be *understood*.”

Nkem felt a coldness settle in her stomach, colder than the silence in her teeth. He was right. And his understanding was the most dangerous thing of all. The forest had shown its hand. It had revealed that its defense was not a blanket negation, but a surgical tool. And a tool could be reverse-engineered. A tool could be hijacked.

The rest of the day passed in a tense, artificial calm. The men worked quietly, using hand-tools to clear the remaining vines, speaking in hushed tones. They set up passive sensors on tripods, listening ears that drank in the silence and tried to measure its depth. Dr. Alabi paced, his mind already elsewhere, composing the report in his head. The report that would not request more anthropologists or ecologists. It would request engineers. Harmonic analysts. Specialists in psychic warfare and electronic countermeasures.

As dusk began to stain the sky the color of bruised fruit, Nkem and Udo slipped away, back toward the town. The path felt different. The familiar sounds—the chatter of insects, the gossip of birds—were muted, hesitant. The forest was exhausted. It had spent its energy on a display of power that had, in turn, made it more vulnerable.

They found Nnenna not in her hut, but at the family shrine at the back of her compound. It was a simple place: a smooth, river-washed stone nestled at the base of the great *ogirisi* tree, surrounded by smaller stones and ancient, chipped clay bowls. She was not praying. She was listening. Her hand rested on the trunk of the tree, her eyes closed.

She spoke without opening them. “The man in the stiff shirt. He has the eyes of a man who looks at a river and sees only the power to turn a mill. He does not hear the river’s song. He calculates the force of its current.”

“He sees a weapon, *Nne*,” Nkem said, the words ash in her mouth. “We showed him how it works.”

Nnenna’s eyes opened. They were deep, cloudy pools reflecting the last of the light. “No, child. You showed him it *could* work. He must now learn the language to speak to it. To give it commands.” She removed her hand from the tree. “That is the next hook. They will try to become the voice. To replace yours.”

Udo sank onto a low stool, his shoulders slumped. “So what do we do? We can’t fight them. They have drones, sensors, dislocators…”

“We do not fight the hook,” Nnenna said, turning to face them. Her voice was the dry click-clack of egusi seeds. “We remind the fish what it is to be a river. The pod is not a thing. It is a memory. And memory is a story. Dr. Alabi wants to turn the story into a manual. We must make the story… inconvenient.”

“How?” Nkem asked.

Nnenna’s gaze settled on her, heavy and direct. “You were the language. The conduit. The artifact spoke through you, to the land. The connection is not broken. It is wounded. It is silent. But a scar is also a connection.” She gestured to the shrine, to the ancient stone. “We have our own stories. Our own memories in the land. Older ones. Deeper ones. The *mmuo* is not one thing. It is a chorus. The pod’s voice is a new song in an old choir. We must remind the choir of its other songs. Songs the man with the tablet cannot hear, because he does not know the tune.”

A plan began to form, not in words, but in a feeling—a dangerous, desperate feeling. It was not about protecting the secret anymore. The secret was out, dissected on a tablet screen. It was about *corrupting the data*. About introducing noise into the clean signal Dr. Alabi sought. To make the forest’s response unpredictable, messy, spiritual in a way that defied his categories.

“The festival of *Agwu*,” Udo said slowly, catching the thread. “It is next moon. The stories of the trickster, the confusion of paths, the speaking in riddles…”

Nnenna gave a slow, almost imperceptible nod. “*Agwu* turns order into chaos. He makes the clear stream muddy. He is the static in the signal. The festival is a story we tell the land, and the land remembers. It plays the story back.”

“But the Authority,” Nkem said. “They have declared martial protocols. They will not allow a gathering. They will see it as a provocation.”

“We will not gather in the town square,” Nnenna said, her voice dropping to a whisper that seemed to blend with the rustling leaves of the *ogirisi*. “We will gather in the stories. In the dreams. In the small, correct rituals at every hearth, at every shrine, at every crossroads. We will not protest. We will… reminisce. We will tell the old, confusing stories of *Agwu* until the air is thick with them. Until the land hears the buzz of a thousand different tunes, and forgets the single, harsh note of the winch.”

It was a guerrilla war of narrative. A spectral insurgency. They would weaponize ambiguity.

That night, Nkem did not sleep. She sat on her mat, listening to the new, fragile quiet. She thought of the dead drone she had carried home, a piece of the sky’s trash. Now, the sky was full of smarter, deadlier drones. And she was to become static. Noise.

She went to the small box where she kept her few treasures. Among them was a single, unbroken rotor from the *Mkpu* drone, its plastic smooth and white. She took it, and a nail, and went outside to the hard-packed earth of the compound. Under the indifferent gaze of a crescent moon, she began to scratch. Not letters. Not patterns. She scratched the feeling of the vine’s thorns scoring grooves. She scratched the hum in her teeth. She scratched the distorted flight of the drone. A chaotic, meaningless sigil.

It was not a message. It was the opposite of a message. It was a scar on the earth, a tiny, intentional flaw. A seed of confusion.

From the veranda, she heard the dry, rhythmic click-clack. Nnenna was shelling seeds in the dark. The sound was a metronome for the coming disorder.

In the distance, from the direction of the grove, a single, powerful spotlight speared the night, illuminating the canopy in an actinic, surgical white. They were working through the night. Mapping the silence. Translating the mystery into a schematic.

Dr. Alabi was writing his report.

And Nkem, with her nail and her piece of plastic, began writing hers. A report in scratches. A story of thorns and static. A hook, not of extraction, but of entanglement.

The winch was silent. But the deeper pulling had just begun. They were no longer trying to remove the splinter. They were trying to graft it to their own nervous system. And the land, in its ancient wisdom, was preparing not to reject, but to *infect*.



---

# Chapter 20

## **CHAPTER 20**

The scratch was a whisper in the skin of the earth. Nkem worked the nail, its tip worn to a dull silver nub, guided by the ghost-light of the moon and the distant, unwavering eye of the spotlight from the grove. The plastic rotor was not a tool, but a compass. She did not drag it; she let its edge find the path of least resistance in the hard laterite, following the memory of vibration in her jaw, the phantom ache of the thorns. The sigil grew, a sprawling, non-Euclidian tangle. It was not meant to be seen. It was meant to be *felt*—a braille of dissonance pressed into the world’s hide.

From the veranda, the click-clack of shelling was a steady, arrhythmic heartbeat. Nnenna did not call out, did not ask. The sound was her acknowledgment. It said: *I am here. The world is ending. The seeds must still be shelled.*

Nkem finished as the moon bled into the milky pre-dawn. Her fingers were cramped, caked with red dust. She stood, her knees popping, and looked at what she had made. It was nothing. It was a wound. It drank the weak light and gave back only a sense of wrong geometry. She spat into its center, a ritual without a name, and turned her back on it.

Inside, the air was cool and still smelled of the previous day’s pepper soup and woodsmoke. She washed her hands, the water in the basin turning a cloudy pink. As she dried them on her wrapper, her gaze fell on the small, locked metal case beneath Nnenna’s sleeping mat. The *Ikenga*. The carved symbol of her grandmother’s will, her right arm. It had not been taken out in weeks. Its absence from the shrine shelf was a louder statement than any prayer. Nnenna was conserving her strength, hoarding her intent. The old woman was a deep aquifer, and the drills were getting closer.
