You built a bridge between two kingdoms that were meant to be forever separate. For that, he considers you more dangerous than the grove. The Pruning is for the network. The directive for the catalyst is… simpler.”

The hologram held her gaze. “They are coming for you. Not with drones. With men. To make an example that does not rely on satellites or sensors. An example that can be understood in every village, in every dialect. Old-fashioned proof.”

The projection vanished. The circle of light on the ground winked out. The *Dike* drone hovered for a three-count, then slid backwards into the tree line, silent as a shadow retracting.

The choice was no longer theoretical. It was a countdown within a countdown.

***

They did not return to Nnenna’s compound. It was the first place the men would come. They went to the old place, the place where it had begun—the gully behind the abandoned cocoa sheds. The *akịrịngba* vine was denser than ever, a furious emerald wall, its thorns like blackened nails. It had already begun to digest the wreckage of the *Mkpu* drone Nkem had found a lifetime ago, weaving carbon-fiber ribs into its own stubborn sinew.

Nnenna was waiting. She had a small fire going, not for warmth, but for light. The egusi seeds were gone. In their place, laid out on a plantain leaf, were objects: a rusted telegraph key, a spindle of fiber-optic cable, a dried *ogilisi* seed pod, and the first, broken photoreceptor lens from that long-dead Messenger.

“Sit,” she said, not as a grandmother, but as a strategist reading a terrain. “The Pruning. They will use the sun.”

Chidi nodded, sinking down by the fire. “Orbital mirrors. Concentrated solar. Surgical, as they said. No explosives, no shrapnel. Just a pinpoint of pure light from heaven, hot as a god’s anger. It will not burn. It will vaporize.”

“Leaving the villages untouched,” Uche spat. “A neat line. A clean cut. To show their mercy.”

“And the men coming for me?” Nkem asked. Her voice was calm. The anxiety had crystallized into a cold, clear substance in her veins.

Nnenna picked up the *ogilisi* pod. She crushed it in her fist. A fine, red powder dusted her palm. “They are the guarantee. If the Pruning fails, if the network fights back from beyond their maps… your body hanging from a tree is a message that needs no translation. It is a period at the end of their sentence.”

The network. It was wounded, but not dead. Grove *Ajofia* was a scorched node, but the signal… the signal had not been a single thread. It was a mycelial web. When the primary cord was severed, the pulse would have echoed, fleeing down secondary pathways, retreating into dormant roots, into the deep mycorrhizal layers, into the silent stones that held water and faint, fossil charge. It was hiding. Licking its wounds. Learning, even now, from its own near-death.

“We have to speak to it,” Nkem said.

Chidi shook his head. “It is in terror. It is a child that has been burned. It will not come to our call.”

“We do not call it,” Nkem said, looking at the objects on the leaf. “We do not translate for it. We give it a new tool. A new word.”

She reached for the telegraph key and the fiber-optic cable. “They are coming with concentrated light. The language of fire from the sky. We cannot fight that light. But we can… borrow it. We can teach the network to see it. Not as destruction, but as a river. A river of pure energy.”

Uche’s eyes widened. “Redirect it? You are mad. It is a scalpel. You cannot catch a scalpel.”

“Not redirect,” Nkem said, her mind moving faster than her words, weaving the fragments together. “*Photosynthesize*.”

The word hung in the smoky air.

Nnenna began to smile. It was a thin, sharp smile. “The first lesson. The very first. You asked the *Mkpu* drone what it was for. It said, ‘To carry messages.’ And you asked the *akịrịngba* vine. And it said, ‘To reach the light.’” She pointed to the thick, thorny wall. “This one. It is greedy for light. It will strangle a full-grown *iroko* to get a sip more sun. It does not fear heat. It craves it.”

The plan was not a plan. It was a metaphor made flesh. A desperate piece of poetry.

They would use the old, half-understood infrastructure. The fiber-optic cable, stolen from a decommissioned Authority data line, was a vein that could carry light. The telegraph key was a switch, a way to tap a simple binary code—a distress pulse, a plea. The *ogilisi* powder was a conductor, a medium to splice organic nerve to synthetic filament. And the *akịrịngba* vine… the *akịrịngba* vine was the receiver. The antenna. The thing that yearned for the sun with a biological lust so pure it was violence.

They would build a prayer. Not to stop the Pruning. But to *invite* it. To aim it.

For the next two days and nights, they worked like surgeons, like saboteurs, like gardeners. Uche and Chidi laid the fiber-optic lines along old animal trails, burying them in shallow trenches, connecting a ring of the most aggressive, light-hungry vine clusters in a five-kilometer radius around the heart of the nearest unburned node—Grove *Iroko*. Nkem and Nnenna worked on the splice. They ground the *ogilisi* seed into a finer paste, mixed it with the cold, conductive sap from a *utazi* root, and used it to fuse the clean-cut ends of the fiber-optic strands to the thick, pulsing vascular bundles they carefully exposed on the main stems of the vines. It was a grotesque grafting. Technology and biology, welded by desperation and vegetable glue.

The telegraph key they connected to a small, solar-powered battery from another scavenged drone. A single button. A single, simple message: *Here. The feast is here.*

It was the night before the Pruning. The air was tense, waiting. The Authority’s evacuation advisories bleated from old radio towers, a robotic litany in three languages. Some families left, their belongings on their heads, their faces blank with a fear they did not fully understand. Many did not. The land was their bones. You do not evacuate your skeleton.

Nkem sat by their crude console—the telegraph key on a flat stone—at the base of the largest *akịrịngba* cluster. The vine seemed to sense the foreign bodies fused to its flesh. It had grown overnight, tendrils curling possessively around the cable, thorns tapping against the glass fiber with a faint, ticking sound.

Nnenna sat beside her, shelling nothing. Her hands were still.

“You remember the burden you brought home?” Nnenna asked, her voice soft.

“The dead drone.”

“It was not dead. It was dormant. Its message was finished. You thought you were saving it. You were reading its epitaph.” She looked at Nkem, her eyes reflecting the starlight. “Tomorrow, you are not saving the land. You are writing an epitaph for a certain kind of war. The war of the scalpel. The war of the clean cut. After tomorrow, the war will be different. It will be messier. It will be in the soil, in the water, in the light itself. There will be no ‘after’ for you. You will be part of the mess.”

“I know,” Nkem said.

“The men with the ropes will still come.”

“I know.”

They lapsed into silence. The grove, the wounded network, was a silent pressure in the dark. It was listening. It was afraid. It was hungry.

***

The Pruning came not at dawn, but at precisely 11:47 AM, when the sun was at its arrogant zenith.

There was no warning siren. Only a change in the quality of the light. The diffuse brightness of the day began to coalesce, to gather itself, as if the sky were a lens focusing. A low hum vibrated in the teeth, in the fillings of Nkem’s molars. It was the sound of satellites aligning, of mirrors the size of football fields angling with inhuman precision.

The first beam struck Grove *Iroko*.

It was not a bolt. It was a column. A solid, blinding rod of distilled sunlight, three meters in diameter. It was soundless. Where it touched the canopy, the leaves did not catch fire. They *disappeared*. Vaporized into a puff of white gas. The trunk beneath followed, sublimating from wood to plasma in a heartbeat. It was terrifying in its cleanliness. A perfect, cylindrical erasure, drilling down from the heavens.

Nkem, watching from a kilometer away through a piece of smoked glass, felt her heart seize. It was one thing to imagine. It was another to see a thousand-year-old tree cease to exist in a silent flash.

But then, the poetry began.

The *akịrịngba* vines, connected by their crude fiber-optic nervous system, felt the surge of energy. Not as destruction, but as a signal. The pulse Nkem had tapped out on the key—*Here. The feast is here*—had been received. The network, hiding in its terror, interpreted the colossal energy signature not as a threat, but as a response. A delivery.

The vines reacted with a single, synchronized, biological imperative: *Consume*.

Tendrils that had been creeping along the forest floor suddenly stiffened. They lifted, not towards the sky, but towards the *beam itself*. They angled the fused tips of their fiber-optic grafts, the *ogilisi*-paste junctions glowing now with an internal, captured radiance.

And they began to *drink*.

It was not a diversion. It was a siphon. A theft.

The perfect, cylindrical beam of solar death, as it drilled into the earth, encountered a voracious, Photosynthetic siphon at its periphery. Filaments of that impossible energy were pulled sideways, into the thirsty vascular bundles of the vines. The light was metabolized. Converted. The vines didn’t burn; they *bloomed*. They exploded in growth, thickening, lengthening, their thorns hardening into obsidian spikes, their leaves broadening into great, light-capturing sails the color of molten emerald.

The Authority’s surgical Pruning beam was being parasitized. Its clean energy was being turned into furious, uncontrolled growth.

The orbital mirror system, detecting an anomalous energy drain and a catastrophic biomass spike exactly where it should be zero, automatically adjusted. It increased power. The beam intensified. The vines drank deeper, grew faster. It was a feedback loop of apocalyptic horticulture.

Grove *Iroko* was gone, vaporized into a deep, smoldering pit. But around the rim of that pit, a monstrous, hyper-evolved thicket of *akịrịngba* now roared into the sky, a tangled, thorny corona sucking at the beam of light, weaving it into new, terrifying forms of itself. Vines as thick as a man’s torso, pulsing with internal, captured sunlight, began to spread radially, moving with a slow, grotesque purpose, seeking more light, more energy.

The Pruning had not cleansed. It had *fertilized*. It had given the network a new word: *Photosynthesis*. And the network, in its raw, biological logic, had conjugated that verb into a weapon of overwhelming, green hunger.

In the control room in Onitsha, Engineer Kwame would be watching his screens in catatonic horror. The telemetry would be nonsensical. The biomass readings were going off the scale. The energy signature was both solar and… biological. The clean cut had unleashed a cancer that fed on the surgeon’s knife.

The man with the flat voice would have his answer. The catalyst had not been neutralized. She had authored a new dialect.

The beam winked out. The satellites, protocols overwhelmed, shut down. The silence that followed was heavier than the hum. In the place of a sacred grove was a geometric scar. And around it, a seething, luminous, semi-synthetic thicket, its every thorn tipped in captured starfire, rustling with a new and terrible kind of awareness.

Nkem lowered the smoked glass. Her ears rang. Her skin tingled with ozone and the backwash of raw creation.

It was then that the men came.

They did not wear Authority uniforms. They wore the drab, practical clothes of laborers. But their movements were not the movements of farmers. They were economical, coordinated. There were six of them. They emerged from the opposite tree line, carrying heavy, old-world rifles and, coiled over one man’s shoulder, a length of coarse, hempen rope.

They saw Nkem, standing alone by the stone with the telegraph key. They saw the monstrous, glowing thicket behind her. A flicker of superstitious fear crossed the leader’s face, but it was quickly buried under flat, professional resolve. The example had to be made. The old way. The understandable way.

Nkem did not run. She turned to face them. She felt the heat from the newborn thicket at her back. She felt the hum of its captured energy in the air. She felt the network’s attention, vast and wounded and now fiercely, greedily alive, focus on this new disturbance.

The leader raised his hand, a signal to fan out.

Nkem took one step backwards, towards the rustling, luminous wall of thorns.

“The war is over,” she said, her voice carrying in the unnatural quiet. “You are not soldiers. You are fertilizer.”

She pressed the telegraph key one last time.

Not a plea. A designation.

The thicket moved. It did not lash out like a whip. It *unfurled*. Tendrils heavy with metabolized sunlight swept forward with a sound like a river of sand. They did not strike the men. They enveloped them. Thorns found not flesh, but the metal of their rifles, the buckles on their packs, the synthetic fibers of their clothes. And they began to feed.

Not on blood. On the minute electrical charges, on the residual heat, on the differential between the warm human bodies and the cool morning air. It was a subtler, more horrifying consumption. The men screamed, not in pain, but in profound, existential violation as their tools, their clothes, the very energy of their living heat was siphoned away, drawn into the glowing, insatiable network. The rifles grew cold, then brittle, then crumbled into flakes of rust. The ropes desiccated, fraying to dust. The men stumbled, weakened, shivering as the warmth was pulled from their very cores.

They broke and ran, not from death, but from being *unmade*.

Nkem watched them go, her breath shallow. The thicket retracted, satisfied, its glow pulsing gently. It had learned another new word: *Circuit*. And it had closed one.

She turned. Nnenna, Chidi, and Uche emerged from their hiding places. Their faces were pale with awe and dread.

It was done. And it was just beginning.

The network was no longer a secret to be protected. It was a fact. A power. It had faced the Authority’s ultimate tool and had not just survived; it had evolved. The anxiety had reached its zenith. The technology they feared was indeed the world itself, awake, intelligent, and now armed with the logic of its own metabolism. The line was gone. The no-man’s-land was everywhere.

Nkem walked to the edge of the new thicket. She knelt. The red mud was hot here, infused with residual energy. It was cool and slick between her fingers. She scooped up a handful. In it, she saw a glint. A tiny, carbon-fiber rib. A thorn. A strand of fiber-optic filament, already being woven into the soil by microscopic rootlets.

The world was translating itself into a new language. A hybrid tongue of root and reason, of thirst and data.

She stood. The others gathered around her, not speaking. There were no words for this. There was only the aftermath, humming in the air.

In the distance, from the direction of Nnenna’s compound, a single, familiar sound cut through the silence. The dry, rhythmic *click-clack* of a calabash. A sound older than the town, older than the country. It was a beat. A heartbeat.

Nnenna heard it too. She nodded, once. “The story is not finished. It has only found a new verse.”

Nkem looked at the monstrous, beautiful, terrifying thicket. She looked at the scorched pit that was Grove *Iroko*. She looked at the sky, now empty of everything but the indifferent sun.

The final chapter was not a battle. It was not a choice.

It was a synthesis.

And she, the translator, was now a permanent part of the text. Her next task was not to interpret between worlds, but to learn how to live in the one she had helped write—a world where the green, ruthless sap and the cold, digital one had mingled in the soil, giving rise to something for which there was not yet a name.

The hook for the final chapter was not in a victory or a defeat. It was in the quiet, relentless *click-clack* from the veranda, a sound weaving the old world and the new into a single, enduring pattern. It was in the knowledge that the men would return, not with ropes, but with a new manual. And the network would be waiting, having already written the first page of its rebuttal.

The rain would fall again. It would fall on hybrid ground. And Nkem would walk through it, barefoot, the red mud cool and slick between her toes, carrying not a dead drone, but the living, breathing, terrifying seed of what was to come.
